Title: Repowering of Wind Turbines in Odsherred Municipality 
 
DTU Wind Energy-M-02866 
June 2019 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
DTU Wind Energy is a department of the Technical University of Denmark 
with a unique integration of research, education, innovation and public/private 
sector consulting in the field of wind energy. Our activities develop new 
opportunities and technology for the global and Danish exploitation of wind 
energy. Research focuses on key technical-scientific fields, which are central 
for the development, innovation and use of wind energy and provides the 
basis for advanced education at the education. 
 
We have more than 240 staff members of which approximately 60 are PhD 
students. Research is conducted within nine research programmes organized 
into three main topics: Wind energy systems, Wind turbine technology and 
Basics for wind energy. 
Project period:  
January 2019 – June 2019 
 
ECTS: 35 
 
Education: Master of Science 
 
 
Supervisors: 
Niels-Erik Clausen, DTU Wind Energy 
 
Simone Veber, Odsherred Municipality

Preface
This thesis is made as a completion of the master education Wind Energy at The Techni-
cal University of Denmark. The authors have a bachelor degree in Mechanical Engineering
from the same university.
Multiple persons have contributed to accomplish the thesis.
We would ﬁrstly like to
thank our supervisor, Niels Erik-Clausen for guiding us through the thesis, which have
been very satisfying. Moreover, we would like to thank the climate coordinator of Odsh-
erred municipality, Simone Grüner Veber Nielsen, for taking her time and providing us
with necessary information and literature regarding the municipality. Thanks to Niels
Gylling Mortensen for helping with the WAsP, WindPRO softwares and achieving data
from the Risø mast.
Thank to all who had taking time to meet with us during the
thesis: Hans-Jørgen Olsen from Geopark Odsherred, Sommerland Sjælland owner Kåre
Dyvekær, and lastly the local grid company Cerius.
Finally thanks to our families and friends who have supported us through the hardest
days.
Risø, June, 2019
Mikail Özcan and Timur Turan.
iii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
The fears of the increase in global temperature is rising which has resulted in various cli-
mate changes around the world. One of the main contributors to the increase is believed
to be the emission of CO2 as a consequence of human actions. A political decision has
been made to decrease the CO2 emissions in the energy sector. Many politicians con-
sider wind energy the solution to this problem, therefore supporting initiatives to solve it.
However, if the wind turbines are placed incorrectly, planning can easily have an adverse
eﬀect, especially in the municipality of Odsherred.
Odsherred is an attractive target for many Danish tourists during the summer. The mu-
nicipality has 25.000 cottages (Danish: sommerhus) registered and is considered to be one
of Denmark’s biggest cottage destinations. The landscape, soil and climate is essential
and originates from the latest ice age. Therefore, the area is widely protected by the
Unesco Geopark of Odsherred, leaving very small pockets of wind turbine siting. In this
thesis, the aim is to help the municipality perceive its strategic energy plan, where one
of the goals is to re-power the existing turbines located in Odsherred. The re-powering
process will be perceived by replacing the existing turbines with modern wind turbines.
To overcome this task, relevant tools and methods in wind turbine planning will be car-
ried out in the study. Communication with relevant stakeholders within tourism will be
made and a beneﬁcial solution for both the municipality and the tourism sector will be
developed.
The results of the study reveal that wind turbine siting can be an achievement even inside
the most unique surroundings and environments. The study will further manifest that
wind energy and tourism can go hand in hand if the correct strategy of communication
with stakeholders is developed.

1. Introduction
The fear of environmental changes due to the increase of global temperature has forced
many nations to think greener. The main contribution to the global temperature rise
is the elevated level of CO2 emission in the atmosphere. In order to limit the change,
it is required to decrease the level of fossil fuel based energy sources which as a result
will lower the CO2 emissions. Through several years, Denmark has been recognized for
its advantages in the wind energy industry and has signed the Paris agreement with the
United Nations in order to limit the global temperature by 1.5 degrees[6]. Since Denmark
is a member state of the European Union (EU) it has to reduce CO2 emissions by 20 %
in the end of 2020[53]. The Danish energy plan is based on this. [12] .
The reduction of CO2 emissions can solve the environmental issue however, on the other
hand give a wide range of political issues both in national and municipal plan. In order
to meet the national goals, the municipality of Odsherred has made an strategic energy
plan (SEP). This plan will help the municipality have fossil free electricity in the heat
sector by the end of 2035 [33]. The energy plan is mainly focused on the energy sector of
the municipality. The most recent goal is to reduce the CO2 emission by more than 20%
by the end of year 2020. In this reduction strategy renewable energy sources are a vital
part and 10% of the reduction is planned to be met with a biogas plant. The other 10%
will be met by replacing the old wind turbines around the municipality.
According to the Danish Energy Agency, the present amount of wind turbines in the
municipality is 30 with a total capacity of 5 MW[1]. The 30 wind turbines can be divided
into two categories in terms of size:
• 15 big turbines: with an average age of 26 years and total capacity of 4850 kW.
• 15 small turbines: with an average age of 21 years and total capacity of 233 kW.
An overview of the present turbines in Odsherred can be seen in ﬁgure 1.1:
1
==PAGE:16==
Figure 1.1: Location of present wind turbines in Odsherred.
According to a report processed by Roskilde University (RUC) 11 turbines out of 30
are ready to be replaced due to two reasons[45].
The ﬁrst reason is the fact that
these turbines have reached their lifetime cycle. The typical lifetime of a wind turbine
is approximately 20 years. The lifetime usually depends on the turbine performance;
the more full load hours the sooner the turbines wear out. The second reason is the
eﬀectiveness and performance of the turbines. Modern turbines are more eﬃcient due to
their height and size as well as the new technologies that allow modern turbines to be
more eﬀective overall. The increased height and size of the turbines will lead to smaller
areas to site since the minimum distance to residential areas has to be greater than four
times the total height of the wind turbine (Danish Ministry of Environment [35]).
Modern wind turbines have diameters up to 120 m , heights of 70-120 m and rated
between 1-4 MW which is considerably larger than the present turbines. This leads to
new challenges in the planning phase, where not only the distance but also the attitude
of the local citizen should be considered. This is due to tourism and change in ownership
models[56].
These factors are important, since Odsherred is considered an attractive
recreational area for many citizens in Zealand.
2
==PAGE:17==
1.1
The Thesis
1.1.1
Objective
By a re-powering of the wind turbines located in the municipality of Odsherred, the aim
is to reduce the CO2 emissions by 20%. Since half of the reduction is expected to be
covered by the future biogas plant, the goal is to reduce the CO2 emissions by 10%. The
municipality consider onshore wind turbines to be challenging in Odsherred due to the
unique landscape of the region, such as the arches and protected habitats, but as well
as the cottage owners, as a result of the large cottage areas. The question is, how is
the required reduction of CO2 emission accomplished considering the political issues the
municipality have? This includes how the replacement of the wind turbines aﬀect the
corresponding stakeholders within tourism. How it is going to aﬀect the landscape and
environment of the unique areas of the municipality and lastly how the stakeholders as
well as the cottage owners can be convinced to accept the proposed project. Since the
thesis is an interdisciplinary project, diﬀerent tools will be introduced in order to solve
the engineering and political issues. Engineering tools will be used to support the achieve-
ment of the political goals described in the Strategic Energy Plan of the municipality. A
short description of the chapters is introduced.
Chapter 2
This chapter elaborates the energy system in Denmark and presents the energy strategy
of the municipality by describing the recent energy savings in the Strategic Energy Plan
(SEP).
Chapter 3
The re-powering of the onshore wind turbines are explained as well as the planning pro-
cesses. Also, the calculation of the needed CO2 reduction in GWh is performed in order
to determine the project size.
Chapter 4
In the Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA), available area for wind turbine siting is
found. The results of the EIA are based on the unique landscapes of the municipality.
The sites are chosen depending on the cottage areas observed due to the municipality
being one of Denmark’s biggest cottage destinations. In addition, factors like churches,
ornithology, protected habitats, laws and regulations on wind turbine planning are pre-
sented. Noise and shadow ﬂicker calculations are made with WindPRO.
3
==PAGE:18==
Chapter 5
In this chapter, the existing turbine site located in Sidinge, is imported to the WAsP
software in order to create a realistic wind ﬂow model of the wind resource assessment
and site assessment. The Annual Energy Production (AEP) of the existing turbines are
estimated and compared with the actual energy production of the turbines which can
be found in the central data register of the Danish Energy Agency. Uncertainty factors
and technical losses are applied with the Wind Assessment Tool (WAT) followed by a
long-term energy correction in order to calibrate the results into more realistic values.
Chapter 6
The most realistic model built in chapter 5 is chosen in chapter 6 and used to calculate
the AEP of the wind turbine sites located in Sidinge Fjord and Fårevejle. The correct
turbine model is selected, based on size and properties.
Chapter 7
The grid connection of the project is studied and explained. The point of connection as
well as the voltage level of the connection is described.
Chapter 8
The project economy includes ﬁnancial assumptions, presentation of project key ﬁnancial
ﬁgures followed by a sensitivity analysis.
Chapter 9
An elaboration of the diﬀerent stakeholders is conducted. Contact to the important stake-
holders is made to understand their attitudes toward the project, but also a beneﬁcial
solution for both, them and the municipality is found. The chapter also includes a short
description of ownership models.
Chapter 10
Chapter 10 contains an alternative solution to the municipality; a hybrid solution which
is a combination of solar and wind power. Also a sensitivity analysis is performed for
diﬀerent solar technologies. As well as this, the economical aspect is studied and com-
pared with wind power economics. The simulation is performed with the PVsyst software.
4
==PAGE:19==
1.2
Wind Turbines
In this section the history of wind turbines, the development and how the wind turbines
work is described.
1.2.1
History and development of Wind Turbines
People have been using wind turbines for thousands of years. As early as 5000 BC the
wind energy was used to propel boats along the Nile river. By 200 BC the wind power was
used to pump water and grind grain[18]. The ﬁrst automatically operating wind turbine
for electricity generation is believed to be the Giant Brush Windmill back in 1887-1888
made by Charles F. Brush. Although the wind turbine had a rotor diameter of 17 m
with an amount of 144 rotor blades, the generator was only a 12 kW model. Later on,
the Dane Poul La Cour was ﬁrst to discover that with faster rotating wind turbines and
a fewer blades the wind turbines would be more eﬃcient. During the world war II the
Danish engineering company F.L Smith built a number of two and three bladed wind
turbines. One of the wind turbines was placed on shallow water in Bornholm and was
the ﬁrst oﬀshore wind turbine placed in the world. Another pioneer worth mentioning
was Johannes Juul, a student of Poul La Cour, which was developing the ﬁrst alternative
current (AC) wind turbines at Vester Egesborg in Denmark. In 1956-1957 the 200 kW
Gedser wind turbine was built by Johannes Juul for the electricity company SEAS. This
wind turbine is considered to be mother of modern wind turbines since it was equipped
with three rotor blades, stall-regulation system and asynchronous motor. These types of
turbines were commonly used until pitch regulated turbines arrived [22].
At the start of the eighties the number of companies that produced wind turbines was
approximately 20, while today only two major companies are left, Vestas and Siemens.
These companies have developed into big multinational entities which arise from the
development in the 1980s. It should be mentioned that the two companies are not pro-
ducing every single part of the wind turbines. Looking at the wind industry we see many
sub-suppliers, it is not only the tower manufacturers, blade manufacturers, control man-
ufacturers, but also a series of companies in the machine industry which deliver anything
from bolts, screws, glass ﬁbre, gears and generators. The biggest sub-supplier in the wind
industry is LM Wind Power, which has delivered blades and brakes worldwide[55]. Today
the biggest wind turbine presenting the highest capacity is the MHI Vestas V164-9.5MW,
with a capacity of 9.5 MW and a rotor diameter of 164 m.
5
==PAGE:20==
1.2.2
Wind Turbine Types
There are two types of wind turbines, horizontal and vertical axis. These types of wind
turbines also come in various designs.
The horizontal axis wind turbine is the most
common type used, all the components are placed in the nacelle (top of the tower). The
blades are faced into the wind and the shaft is horizontal to the ground, this type of
turbine also has some key features that add to its eﬃciency and safety. The blades can
be pitched to reach an optimal angle of attack and can even be used as a brake to rotate
the blade out of the wind when it needs to be shut down. Another feature is the yawing
where the top of the tower can be rotated so the hub will face the wind, receiving a
better eﬃciency. Furthermore this type of turbine has measurement components such as
a wind vane that measures where the wind is coming from and a cup anemometer which
measures the wind speed. These measuring components are monitored by a controller in
order to give the wind turbine the best power output. In ﬁgure 1.2 a horizontal wind
turbine can be seen with its main components. Some wind turbines are designed so that
the blades are facing the wind. These are called upwind turbines. On the other hand the
downwind turbines have the wind passing the tower before it strikes the blades.
Figure 1.2: Sketch of a horizontal axis wind turbine[59].
For the vertical axis wind turbine the shaft connecting the blades is vertical to the ground,
unlike the horizontal axis where the main components and the turbine itself are placed
close to the ground. The vertical axis has two diﬀerent types of turbines, one is drag
based while the other is lift based. The lift based type is more eﬃcient than the drag
based type[59]. The advantages of the vertical axis are its easy maintenance and the low
construction and transportation costs. The vertical axis is less eﬃcient compared to the
horizontal axis and the blade is constantly spinning in the wind causing it to drag. The
vertical axis wind turbine for lift based type can be seen in ﬁgure 1.3.
6
==PAGE:21==
Figure 1.3: Lift based vertical axis wind turbine[59].
Wind turbines are also produced with various technologies depending on the models
where turbines with gearbox or direct drive are applicable. For example for a 1-MW
wind turbine gearbox the blades rotating with 15-20 RPM are converted into about 1800
RPM in the generator to produce the electricity. These causes tremendous stresses for
the wheels and the bearings in a gearbox and at oﬀshore these stresses would increase
even more due to faster wind speeds. Therefore direct drive technologies, wind turbines
without gearbox, have started to make the way in the wind industry. These are praised for
their design and are less complex compared to the gearbox resulting in easier maintenance
and operation[16].
1.2.3
How Wind Turbines Work
The wind turbines operate on a simple principle where wind blows toward the rotor
blades causing the rotor and then central drive shaft to rotate. With the large modern
wind turbines a pitch mechanism helps the blades to face the wind at the best angles to
get an optimal power output. Then, there is the gearbox which converts the low-speed
rotation into high speed rotation to drive the generator. It is the generator that produces
the electricity by taking the kinetic energy from the spinning shaft and turning it into
electrical energy. The measurement components cup anemometer and wind vanes are
used to control the wind turbine to extract the maximum amount of energy as well as for
safety reasons mentioned in section 1.2.2. The electric current produced by the generator
7
==PAGE:22==
is then ﬂowing through a cable inside the tower and down to the step-up transformer.
The transformer then converts the electricity to approx. 50 times higher voltage in order
to transmit the electricity to the power grid more eﬃciently. Lastly the electricity is
delivered to the households through the grid[49]. The components of a wind turbine are
illustrated in ﬁgure 1.4.
Figure 1.4: Illustration of how a wind turbine work. Source: Mechanical Eng.com
Even at very small wind speeds as 3-4 m/s the wind turbine starts to rotate and generate
electrical power. This wind speed interval is called the cut-in speed. The cut-out speed
of around 25 m/s is where the wind turbine stops. If the forces on the turbine structure
continue to rise, the risk of rotor damage will arise. To stop the wind turbine, a braking
system is employed. When the wind speed is increased from the cut-in speed, the electrical
output power increases rapidly until it reaches the rated wind speed. At this point, the
power output has reached it’s maximum and will be constant until cut-out speed[43].
The rated wind speed usually lies between 12-17 m/s.
1.3
Odsherred Municipality
This section consists of a description of the municipality. Odsherred is located in the
Northwestern area of Zealand and has a population of 32.466 inhabitants registered in
2013. With an area of 355 km2 and a total of 25.000 cottages, Odsherred is one of the
cities with most cottages in Denmark. The total number of households in Odsherred
are 15.726 (in 2012). When comparing the amount of households with the cottages, the
population indicates that the cottages are not inhabited the whole year.
8
==PAGE:23==
1.3.1
City areas in Odsherred
In the municipality there are diﬀerent areas which are known for their diﬀerent features.
In ﬁgure 1.5 a map of Odsherred is shown with the city areas. As seen in the ﬁgure the
municipality is surrounded by the ocean for most of its territory.
Figure 1.5: Map over the areas of Odsherred Municipality [28]
Havnebyen
In Havnebyen and the surrounding areas villages like Yderby Lyng and Overby Lyng
are situated. Here you can ﬁnd cottage areas and a ferry harbour. The population of
this area is 699 people (2012) [33]. The area is a long isthmus with sea on both sides.
The isthmus is low-lying with a small terrain fall towards the shore. Along the coast of
Havneby there are small hills and the soil is mostly sand-mixed clay soil[37].
Lumsås and Stenstrup
Lumsås, Stenstrup and the surrounding areas have a small population of 120 people.
However, many cottage areas can be found here as well as one of the biggest pharmacy
company, Lundbeck. Lumsås which is known for its notable eskers is a part of a larger
9
==PAGE:24==
edge moraine system. The area has troubled terrain, steep sides along with many drainage
holes[37].
Rørvig
Rørvig and the surrounding areas are located in the Northeastern corner of the munic-
ipality. It houses a population of 1.037 and has a ferry harbour. The central part of
Rørvig is an ice age landscape steep, fossil cliﬀs whereas the surrounding marine suburbs
are dominated by beach ravines with shrunk beach lakes along the north and east coast.
[37].
Egebjerg
In Egebjerg the population is 406 people.
This is the ﬁrst elected local town of the
municipality and has 7 small villages. The landscape on Egebjerg was formed when the
ice melted after the last ice age. The area has many small lakes and run-oﬀhollows
originated from pieces of ice that have been left behind after the ice was withdrawn. The
terrain is predominantly wavy and considerably hilly. The area is in general elevated with
a smooth fall towards the coast and the reclaimed Sidinge Fjord. The more hilly areas lie
primarily near the coast and are gathered in a stroke of hill sections between both Bråde
and Egebjerg [37].
Nykøbing Sjælland
The biggest city in the municipality is Nykøbing Sjælland with a population of 5747
inhabitants. Here there are cottage areas and a marina. The soil is mostly ﬁnely ground
on the lower-laying areas, except to the east of Nykøbing, where the land is dominated
by clay soil, sandy soil and sand-mixed clay soil on the higher situated moraine and dead
ice. [37].
Højby
The central region of the municipality where the town hall is located is named Højby.
There is an amusement park called Sommerland Sjælland which attracts a lot of tourists
during the summer. The area has a population of 1.465. The characterizing elements
appear clearly in the area, in the form of large hills and hollows, intensively cultivated
ﬁelds, partially overgrown fences and dikes, high-altitude villages and scattered houses
and farms. The cultural history of the character origin, with replaced farms, is evident[37].
10
==PAGE:25==
Vig
Vig is located in the middle of the municipality and has a population of 1.516. In this
area cottages are present. The city has a special cultural identity in the municipality
based on market activities, festivals and a movie theater which is the only one in the
municipality[37].
Grevinge and Herrestrup
At the border of Holbæk cities of Grevinge, Herrestrup can be found with a population
of 917. Here a church and station are located. The landscape is characterized by a strong
wavy to hilly terrain and intensively cultivated ﬁelds with scattered vegetation on the
ground as well as many scrub land hollows. Small high-laying villages, characterized by
farms, are characteristic for the area along with scattered houses along roads[37].
Asnæs
One of the last main cities of the municipality is Asnæs. The city is located in the South-
western part and has a population of 2.870. Here a city mall and diﬀerent institutions
can be found. The landscape around Asnæs is created by glaciers. The ice has oﬀset the
contours of the landscape, and when it melted, it gave the region a wavy terrain. The ice
also left larger ice blocks which created water-ﬁlled hollows. They appear today as small
overgrown lakes and water-ﬁlled inclines [37].
Vallekilde and Hørve
At the end of the Southwestern part, Vallekilde, Hørve and the surrounding areas are
located. This area boards the two cities of Kalundborg and Holbæk with a population of
2.379. The landscape here is wavy with large hills in the central and eastern part and can
be characterized by wet hollows. Towards South and Northeast, the terrain rises from
the wavy surface to edge moraine shapes [37].
Fårevejle Kirkeby and Fårevejle Stationsby
Lastly there is Fårevejle Kirkeby and Fårevejle Stationsby laying in the Southern part
of the municipality.
The population is 2.534 and has a wide commercial area which
borders Holbæk in the Southeast area. The soil conditions on the reclaimed fjord ﬂoor
vary in zones with sand, clay and rock in levels between 0 and 3 m. The fjord previously
contained four small islands. Today, their sandy beach zone is evident in and around
Fårevejle station town. The terrain is ﬂat with single swings at the small islands [37].
11
==PAGE:26==
1.3.2
Tourism strategy in Odsherred
Odsherred has 25.000 cottages which makes them one of the greatest vacation destinations
in Denmark. This leads to a great focus on making the municipality more attractive as
it is an economic impetus. Therefore, the municipality has made a tourism strategy to
increase growth and job opportunities. The yearly turnover in the municipality is in the
order of 1.6-1.7 billion which corresponds to 3.6 million days of accommodation[41]. The
statistical ﬁgures also indicate how dependant the municipality is on tourism. Some of
the important attractions besides the cottages is Sommerland Sjælland and Dragsholm
Castle. With the appointment of Odsherred as UNESCO Global Geopark in 2015 the
municipality has a great potential for further development. Since the tourism is only
at it’s peak during the summer the municipality has an interest to extent the period
where tourists visit the municipality which will therefore increase the amount of visitors.
The tourist board (Visit Odsherred) has on that basis made strategies to make sure the
tourism will continue to develop. The Ministry of Industry, Business and Financial Aﬀairs
and the Local Government Denmark (KL) made an agreement that aims for a reduction
in tourist attractions by 2021. There should be between 15 to 25 destinations to visit
in Denmark, which is a reduction of the already existing destinations. Visit Odsherred
has made four major eﬀorts to increase the growth and job opportunities. Their main
goal is to create 100-150 full-time equivalents (FTE) during the tourism strategy period
applicable from 2019-2024. In table 1.1 an overview of the actions that are planned to
be made from Visit Odsherred along with the expected outcome is illustrated[41].

==PAGE:27==
The Odsherred Geopark and Visit Odsherred
The municipality’s representatives within tourism are Geopark and the tourist board Visit
Odsherred. Geopark is a land area with a special geology and can best be compared to
a natural park or a national park.
The area of Geopark is equal to the area of the
municipality. The Geopark concept is to create development and growth for and with
the local population.
It can be in the form of increased focus on sustainability and
geotourism. The back bone of Geopark Odsherred is the Odsherred arches (Vejrhøjbuen,
Vigbuen and Højbuen), which were formed during the last ice age. The formations in
Odsherred are a school example of a landscape created by the ice. They are the reason why
Geopark Odsherred is part of the European and Global Geoparks Network, respectively
the European and the worldwide network of Geoparks under the protection of UNESCO
[19].
Figure 1.6: The Odsherred Arches source: Odsherred Geopark
The collaboration and division of labor between Geopark Odsherred and Visit Odsherred
is important. The role of the Geopark is primarily to anchor the whole concept and
to explore and convey the landscape and its connection within art, cultural history and
raw materials. Also to protect the characteristic landscape of the municipality. Visit
Odsherred’s role is to realize the commercial potential through business development and
13
==PAGE:28==
communication / marketing as well as to ensure that the tourism actors are dressed to
deliver competent hosting - both physically and digitally [40].
The project will have a special focus on tourism during the re-powering process of the
existing wind turbines. A brief study of every area in the municipality will be conducted
and its importance to the Geopark and tourism will be considered before the siting and
planning of the wind turbines.
14
==PAGE:29==
2. The Energy System
In this chapter the energy system and politics will be described brieﬂy on national level
and a detailed description for the municipality of Odsherred along with the Strategic
Energy Plan (SEP) for Odsherred municipality.
2.1
The Danish energy system
In order to understand the energy system in Odsherred municipality, the energy system in
Denmark is described, as Odsherred municipality is a part of the Danish Energy System.
The energy politics began as a political area in the rise of the oil crisis in the 1970’s,
where the focus was on the energy prices and the security of supply. Later the dimension
of the climate was added due to the high emissions from the industry.
Most of the
Danish energy and climate policy is largely based on the commitments taken on in an
international and European context where some of the objectives originate from the UN.
In addition general EU regulations are adopted, which has direct eﬀect in Denmark.
2.2
Energy politics in Denmark
In this section the energy politics in Denmark is described with targets for 2020. As
a member of the European Union (EU), Denmark is obligated to achieve the goals set
by the EU. One of the main targets in Denmark is to reduce the CO2 emissions by
20 % compared to the CO2 level of 2008.
These should be reduced from buildings,
agriculture and transportation.
The fraction of renewable energy in the total energy
consumption should be covered by 30 % in 2020. Lastly the fraction of renewable energy
should be covered by 10 % in the transport sector by 2020 [13]. The greenhouse gas
emissions is expected to be reduced by 38-39 % by 2020. The renewable shares are then
expected to increase to 43.6 % up to 2021, followed by a decline due to the increase in
electricity consumption. This will also increase the emissions after 2021. The rise in
electricity consumption will mainly be from data centres which will account for 65 % of
the increase[2].
15
==PAGE:30==
2.3
The Energy System in Odsherred
In Odsherred municipality oil heating represents 25 % of the current heating source. If
the plan of phasing out the oil burner is implemented the municipality will have some
challenges in the future. Another heating source widely used in the municipality is based
on district heating from a natural gas ﬁred burner, where none or minor investments
have been made to change over to renewable energy[33]. One of them is the bio-gas plant
which is going to be installed.
2.4
Energy politics in Odsherred
In this section the energy politics are described. Mostly the objectives are closely related
to the international and national politics (section 2.1). Since the municipality has chosen
to participate in the Covenant of Mayors, the municipality is bound to reduce the CO2
emissions by 20% compared to the level of 2008.
A new pact will be considered for
signing by the city council in September 2019. Moreover, the municipality has made
an agreement with The Danish Society for Nature Conservation in order to reduce the
CO2 emission by 2% per year until 2025[33]. In table 2.1 an overview of the energy plan
objectives can be seen. This project only focuses on the objective including the CO2
reduction of 20 % by 2020.
Energy Plan Objectives for Odsherred Municipality
100% renewable energy in electricity and heat source by 2035.
100% renewable energy in the whole energy system by 2050.
The municipality’s own CO2 emissions should be lowered by 2%
per year until 2025.
Oil-ﬁred burners must be phased out by 2030.
Energy supply, including industry and transport must be covered by
renewable energy in 2050.
Energy consumption must be reduced by 8% compared to 2010
before 2020.
At least 50% of livestock manure is to be used for biogas by 2020.
Reduction of CO2 emissions by 20 % compared to the level of 2008 by 2020.
Table 2.1: Table over the energy plan objectives for Odsherred municipality[33]
16
==PAGE:31==
2.4.1
Strategic Energy Plan
The purpose of the SEP in Odsherred municipality is to work towards a more ﬂexible
energy system with less energy consumption and more renewable sources. This will be
done in order to fulﬁll the requirements of the energy area which were put upon the
municipality by the international and national objectives. The SEP contributes to an
overview over the various initiatives made by the municipality in order to achieve the
objectives. It is important that the eﬀorts also includes areas such as, sustainable tourism,
local commitment to energy renovations, business development and settlement.
The
SEP can include citizens and the companies energy consumption, the energy production
in general, transport and the possibility to use waste for the energy production.
In
Odsherred there is a lot of biomass including slurry, straw, deep litter, seaweed, grass
and wood.
Therefore it would be advantageous for the municipality to use the local
resources for the energy and heat production[33]. An overview of the energy savings the
municipality have planned can be seen on the table in appendix O. The table has been
imported from the strategic energy plan [33].

==PAGE:32==
3. The Planning and Development Pro-
cesses
The planning process and development of wind turbines and wind farms can be very
challenging as it covers various activities consisting:
• Wind resource
• Environmental impact
• Public acceptance
• Grid connection
• Project economy
These are called the ﬁve cornerstones of wind farm planning and development and are
described in details in the course notes [7].
Before going into details on how the planning of the project should be, it is necessary
to know what is exactly required. It is necessary to meet the remaining 10% reduction
of CO2 emission. The question is how big the energy production should be to cover this
reduction which will be answered in the upcoming section. Then potential areas of the
municipality will be investigated by taking diﬀerent parts of the Environmental Impact
Assessment (EIA) into consideration. These include visual impact as well as landscape
and laws within diﬀerent sectors that can have an impact on determining the potential
sites for the turbines. These laws can describe what distances are required from public
and residential areas such as churches, cottages, protected areas, beaches and coastal
areas to the desired wind turbines. Lastly, noise and shadow ﬂickering will be computed
with the WindPRO software.
The wind resource assessment will be treated by the use of wind resource and siting
software WAsP. The features and applications of the software as well as the inputs will
be described in detail. The grid connection will also be elaborated in the planning process
as well as the project economy.
3.0.1
CO2 Calculation
In this section a calculation of the needed energy production will be made in order to
reduce the CO2 emission by 10 %.
18
==PAGE:33==
To reduce the CO2 emission by 20% the municipality has to reduce the amount of CO2
by 70.759 tonnes of CO2 equivalents. The biogas plant will cover half of the reduction,
meaning 10% reduction of CO2 emissions is left to be covered by the upcoming wind
turbines. This means that the reduction of CO2-emissions has to be 35.380 tonnes of
CO2 equivalents. Figure 3.1 shows the average values for diﬀerent fossil fuels per kWh.
Figure 3.1: Table over emissions for diﬀerent fossil fuels. Source Energinet[54]
Based on the previous investigations made by the University of Roskilde it has been
found out, that there are 11 wind turbines that potentially could be replaced by new
wind turbines[45]. The total sum of energy production for 11 turbines in 2018 is then
added to MWh needed in order to comply with the CO2 reductions. The new modern
wind turbines usually have a full load hour of 2200 h[58]. This assumption will be used
for the calculations of the CO2 reduction, together with the average emissions of coal
taken from table 3.1.
The amount of MWh needed per year would then be:

fulﬁll the CO2 reductions. This leads to an AEP of 53107 MWh

In ﬁgure 3.2 it is possible to see the location of the 11 wind turbines from table 3.1. It is
important to mention that once the turbines are decommissioned it would not necessarily
mean that they can be replaced on the same locations. This is because modern turbines
are bigger in height and size, meaning that the old distance requirements are not valid
anymore. The distance requirement from residential areas to wind turbines is deﬁned as
four times the total height of the wind turbine. New locations are obligated to be found,
moreover a new EIA-report is necessary to be conducted before turbine siting.

4. Environmental Impact Assessment
4.1
Introduction
The environmental impact assessment of the municipality will be used to discover the
best areas to site the project.
The areas were found by including Danish laws and
regulations in the ﬁeld of wind energy. Many of them can be found in the plandata
map[17]. In addition, protected areas, churches, lakes, parks and more related matters
within Denmark can be found. These are implemented into Google Earth where areas
for both 150 m and 100 m wind turbines are located. The Google Earth map showing
areas for 100 m WTs can be found in appendix F.1.
Figure 4.1: Potential wind turbine locations for 150m height wind turbines
In ﬁgure 4.1 potential areas to site the wind turbines are illustrated by white shaded
areas. The laws and regulations within wind farm planning have been applied to locate
these areas. The red circles indicate the distance requirement needed from a residential
to a wind turbine. The requirement can be found in table 4.1. The green circles are
forest protection lines since the wind turbines have to be placed 300 meters away from
forests. Stream of water protection lines are shown with the yellow lines. A wind turbine
22
==PAGE:37==
must not be located above this protection line which is 150 m away from a stream of
water. Other watercourses such as rivers or lakes are shown with the blue protection
line. Like the stream of water line, wind turbines must be placed 150 m away from these
areas. The green protection line indicates protected habitats. There is no restriction and
distance requirement to protected habitats but it can still be signiﬁcant for the project.
According to §3 in the Nature Protection Act, it is not prohibited to change 1) natu-
ral lakes if the area is over 100 m2 . The same applies to stream of waters which by
The Ministry of Environment designated as protected [35]. 2) When habitats such as
heaths, marshes, salt marshes and beach swamps, as well as fresh meadows and biologi-
cal excesses individually, together, or in connection with the lakes mentioned in (1) are
larger than 2500 m2 in continuous area or bogs which are less than 2500 m2 when in con-
nection with a lake or a watercourse the protection will be very restrictively managed [35].
The purple protection line stipulates birds and bats. It is seen in both ﬁgures that the
bird protection line is only visible close to the town of Nakke in the Northern part of
the municipality. The reason behind crowded bird species in this area is due to Dybesø,
which is an old protected lake close to Kattegat [37]. The area is also considered to be
a Natura 2000 area. Special rules apply for the planning and permission for projects,
including wind turbines, in or near international nature conservation areas (Natura 2000
areas). The habitats order §5 includes prohibition on planning wind turbines near Natura
2000 areas in order to protect special bird species [35]. The orange protection line shows
earth and stone walls. According to the museum Act §5 it is prohibited to change the
condition of earth and stone walls [35]. The yellow circles are the distance from churches.
Buildings with a height above 8.5 m must not be built within a distance of 300 m from
a church, unless the church is surrounded by urban buildings throughout the protection
zone [35]. Ancient monuments are shown with pink circles. According to §18 in the Nature
Conservation Act constructions including wind turbines must be placed 100 meters away
from ancient monuments [35]. The red shaded area at both maps is where the bio-gas
plant is going to be installed. Cultural-historical preservation values are shown with the
light green shaded areas. Cultural environments often interact with their surroundings,
including the landscape, where possibly wind turbines are planned. The churches appear
to be distinctive cultural-historical landmarks in the landscape [35]. Nature conservation
areas are shown with dark green shaded areas. In these areas it is now allowed to place
wind turbines [35], while potential nature conservation areas are highlighted with very
light green colored areas [35]. An overview of the described distance requirements can be
found in table 4.1

analysis of the municipality [37].

show fewer disadvantages. The region is fairly uncrowded and compared to the others it
has the fewest amount of cottages. The territory is large and can room a number of wind
turbines. Fårevejle and Hagesholm are potentially good locations to site. Both have few
cottage areas and a larger area to site the wind turbines. These three regions are chosen
and will be described in more details in the upcoming sections.

4.3.1
Visual Impact and Landscape
A very ﬂat landscape is observed due to the previously existing fjord. The area is used
for agriculture and the closest town Egebjerg is located 2.4 km away. Beside the coast,
a narrow road leads to the town of Egebjerg. Due to the open land the turbines will
be very visible from the road. To have an idea of the visual impact of the turbines,
images from the road and the cottages have been taken. These areas are considered to
have most visual impact. With the photo montage tool in WindPro it is possible to
generate a rendered photo of the turbines from a given location. The captured images
have been used to generate such photos. To give the turbines a realistic appearance, a
post-processing of the photos has been made with Photoshop. Other photos which were
captured during the investigation of the area can be found in appendix N.
Figure 4.3: Visual Impact of the turbines seen from the road which leads to the town of Egebjerg. The wind turbines are
very visible due to the open land.
In ﬁgure 4.3, the photo has been captured from the road. The appearance of the turbines
is large due to the open landscape. The layout of the turbines is based on the noise
calculation of the area explained in section 4.3.3. The location of the turbine sites can
be seen in ﬁgure 6.2.

The size of the wind turbines might aﬀect the churches located nearby. Mentioned in

landscape and can play a signiﬁcant role in the approval of the project. From ﬁgure
4.2 the distances from each church located in Vig and Egebjerg are shown respectively.
According to green churches in Denmark, the church located in Egebjerg is a member
of the Green Church Organization. The purpose of this organization is to inspire and
motivate individuals, churches and church organizations to take responsibility for the
climate and environmental challenges the world is facing [27]. The church located in
Egebjerg is not considered to be an obstacle due to its membership in the Green Church
Organization. The church in Vig is located far from the site to be considered and is
believed to not have an eﬀect on the project progress. However, it is not excluded to
keep the contact with the churches during the planning since they still play a important
role in project acceptance.
4.3.2
Shadow ﬂicker
Shadow ﬂickering is an important subject to consider that primarily aﬀects the neigh-
bouring area. The shadow ﬂickering can cause discomfort for the local citizens when
present.
The ﬂicker occurs due to meteorological conditions including sun and wind
which is seasonal. Furthermore the number of wind turbines and the location according
to the neighbouring residents play a major role in the amount of shadow ﬂickering. For
detailed description of the shadow ﬂicker see [7]. In Denmark, however, there are no
requirements regarding shadow ﬂickering, though the shadow ﬂicker should not exceed
10 hours/year according to the guidelines. In Sidinge Fjord calculations of the shadow
ﬂicker is carried out for 3 and 4 wind turbines respectively. This is done through the
software WindPRO. In order to compute the shadow ﬂicker, the Danish sun statistics
gained from Planstyrelsen and the online data from MERRA 2 has been used as input.
Furthermore the wind turbines are placed in the desired positions and the receptors at the
coordinates where the residents are present. The elevation has also been accounted for
as it plays a role for the shadow ﬂicker. In order to avoid shadow ﬂicker from non-visible
turbines a ZVI calculation is made beforehand. The receptor that represents the window
of the residents has a height and a width of 1 m with an elevation of 1 m. Also the
window is positioned vertically at 90◦. These input parameters are used for all sites in
the following calculations of shadow ﬂicker along with the Siemens SWT-4.0-120 turbine
unless otherwise is speciﬁed.
29
==PAGE:44==
In ﬁgure 4.6 the results for the shadow ﬂicker are shown. The numbering 1-3 illustrates the
turbines while the letters represents the neighbouring residents and the colours indicates
the amount of ﬂicker. The shadow ﬂicker does not exceed 10 hours/year at any residents
thus complying with the guidelines. By detailed inspection of the results it is noticed
that the resident denoted by T on the ﬁgure has the highest amount of shadow ﬂicker
reaching 8.24 hours/year and needs close intention, whereas the other residents do not
exceed more than 4 hours/year. For detailed results and visualization of turbines and
residents see appendix D.1.1.
Figure 4.6: Shadow ﬂicker for 3 WT in Sidinge
For the results regarding the shadow ﬂicker of 4 wind turbines refer to appendix D.1.1.
30
==PAGE:45==
Mitigation
A way of mitigating the shadow ﬂicker is to turn of the wind turbines in the peak hours
where the sun is shining. The new modern wind turbines are equipped with the tech-
nology to automatically shut down during these peak hours, known as the shadow ﬂicker
protection system.
4.3.3
Noise
One of the side eﬀects originating from the wind turbines is the noise emitted from
turbines. As many citizens located near wind turbines may be disturbed by the noise,
it is important that the noise requirements are respected. The emitted noise from the
turbines comes from the wind interacting with the rotor blades and is often noticeable
periodically.
This is called the aerodynamic noise.
There is also a mechanical noise
emitted from the turbines however this is not of signiﬁcance as the emitted sound is very
small compared to the aerodynamic noise. For further explanation of noise see [7]. In
table 4.3 an overview of the noise requirements for Denmark is shown. For open land the
noise emission can be greater than for residential areas.
6 [m/s]
8 [m/s]
Open land
42 dB(A)
44 dB(A)
Residential
area
37 dB(A)
39 dB(A)
Table 4.3: Noise requirements from wind turbines in Denmark measured at outdoor living areas[30].
In order to distinguish between open land and residential areas the guidelines from the
Environmental Protection Agency have been used.
These state that if more than a
handful residents located in a short distance between them the area is considered as a
residential area[35]. Figure 4.7 shows the noise results for the Sidinge Fjord where noise
is emitted from 3 turbines. The colours illustrates the level of noise at the area, while the
residents are denoted with a letter. The noise is calculated at wind speeds of 10 m height
and is done for 6 m/s and 8 m/s respectively. Further the low frequency noise must
not exceed 20 dB indoor at the these wind speeds. The mentioned noise requirements
do not apply for individuals which are wind turbine owners. In order to compute the
noise emitted from the wind turbines the software WindPRO has been used. The wind
turbines are placed together with the noise sensitive areas representing the residents and
the wind data from MERRA 2 is used as input. In addition, the roughness and elevation
is also accounted for. The method of noise calculation is the Dansk 2011 and 2015 from
The Ministry of Environment.
31
==PAGE:46==
(a) Noise results at 6 m/s
(b) Noise results for 8m/s
Figure 4.7: Noise emission from the 3 wind turbines located in Sidinge Fjord
Figure 4.7(a) illustrates the noise results for 6 m/s as it is observed how the noise level
is lower compared with the results for the wind speed of 8 m/s ﬁgure 4.7(b). It is stated
that for the 3 Siemens SWT-4.0-120 turbines all noise requirements are respected. Since
the surrounding area of Sidinge Fjord consists of a lower amount of residents and most
of the residents are far away from each other, most of the residents are deﬁned as open
land making therefore it more easy to comply with the requirements.
Mitigation
Although the noise requirements are kept, the mitigating measure is worth mentioning
in order to get the public acceptance. Oﬀering shares of the turbines to those living in
the vicinity of the wind turbines could decrease the annoyed noise as studies conducted
in Netherlands indicates[7].
4.4
Fårevejle
In the southern part of the municipality Fårevejle Kirkeby and Fårevejle Stationsby are
located. This region of the municipality is known for it’s cultural and historical values.
In the Fårevejle area two churches are present one of which is situated inside Dragsholm
32
==PAGE:47==
Castle as illustrated in ﬁgure 4.8. The church located in the Dragsholm Castle as well
as the castle itself is one of Denmark’s oldest castles and has been located in Odsherred
for about 800 years [48]. As highlighted with the white shaded color in ﬁgure 4.8, a
large area is available to site the wind turbines. At the same time it is placed within the
green shaded area which according to plandata is reserved as a cultural and historical
preservation value and can be due to the appearance of the castle and Fårevejle church.
In addition, the available area is close to the Odsherred arches described in 1.3.2, which is
important to the Geopark and the Dragsholm Castle. Even if the area is placed outside
the arches, contact with the Odsherred Geopark is necessary in order to avoid issues
during the planning phase. This subject will be elaborated later in chapter 9.
Figure 4.8: Available area to site in Fårevejle
According to the SEP of Odsherred, Fårevejle Kirkeby has 735 inhabitants while Fårevejle
Stationsby has 1799 in 2012. Beside the local town of Fårevejle Kirkeby, ﬁve villages are
present as well as four cottage areas near the Sejerø Bay; Veddinge Bakker, Bjergene,
Ordrup, Næs and Knarbo Klint [33]. Lastly, three Nordtank wind turbines are located
Southeast of the area. Information regarding these turbines is likely to be found in table
3.1.
4.4.1
Visual Impact and Landscape
The area is likewise Sidinge Fjord used for agriculture and was a part of the huge fjord
called lammefjorden which is explains the observed ﬂat terrain. Images captured during
the investigation of the area can be found in appendix N. The area is placed between
33
==PAGE:48==
Fårevejle and Fårvejle Stationsby. The four cottage areas mentioned in 4.4 are located
too far away to be considered.
The biggest challenge in this area, regarding the acceptation of the project are the castle
and the church.
Figure 4.9: Rendered photo of the wind turbines seen from Fårevejle church
The Fårevejle church is located 1.3 km away from the area. In ﬁgure 4.9 the visual impact
of the turbines from the church can be seen. Both of the turbines are visible. Due to the
cloudy sky the turbines are blurred but are to be clearer in clear sky. The landscape seen
from the church is therefore harmed, but not as much as expected. It was believed, that
the visualizations of the turbines would be much larger in the landscape to start with.
However, it is still important to achieve their acceptance of the project since the church
can apply veto due to the minimum distance requirement not being respected.
The Dragsholm castle is located 2.6 km away from the area. It has a lot of visitors yearly
and recognized as travellers’ number one choice according to Trip Advisor. It is a part
of the UNESCO Geopark in Odsherred and described as a symbol of masterpiece of the
Danish Kingdom. It is praised for its incredible landscape of the Lammefjorden and its
fascinating garden. The castle also acts as a hotel and is a member of the prestigious
hotel association Relais and Châteaux[46]. The hotel provides activities such as hunting,
hiking ﬁshing and horse riding [46].
34
==PAGE:49==
Figure 4.10: The garden of Dragsholm Castle.
In ﬁgure 4.10, it is possible to see the landscape from the castle. The point of view is
toward the area seen in 4.8. The photo montage tool was used for this case but the
rendered turbines lied behind the trees of the garden. The turbines are not visible from
the garden. Hence, the only issue in Fårvejle regarding the landscape is seen from the
Fårevejle church.
4.4.2
Shadow Flicker
In this section the shadow ﬂicker is investigated for Fårevejle with two wind turbines.
Figure 4.11 shows the results of shadow ﬂickering and it is observed how little the ﬂickering
aﬀects the nearby residents. From the detailed results which can be seen in appendix
D.2 it is noticed that many of the residents have zero shadow ﬂicker while the others are
aﬀected by a small amount of ﬂicker close to zero hour/year. The only two residents that
are aﬀected by the ﬂicker are the residents located at O and AE, however, the amount of
ﬂicker is very low with a shadow ﬂicker no more than 3 hour/year.
35
==PAGE:50==
Figure 4.11: Shadow ﬂicker for 2 WT in Fårevejle
The results comply with the guidelines of shadow ﬂicker and it is stated that Fårevejle
has a great potential in the planning of a wind turbines based on the shadow ﬂicker.
4.4.3
Noise
In Fårevejle the noise calculations are carried out for the same two turbines that were
used for the calculations in shadow ﬂicker. The results which can be seen in ﬁgure 4.12 for
both 6 and 8 m/s show that the noise requirements are met. Most of the areas are deﬁned
as open land and only two areas are deﬁned as residential areas. The detailed results
seen in appendix D.2 show that the turbines can be moved more than 400 m closer to the
residential areas and still comply with the requirements. Analyzing the noise emission for
36
==PAGE:51==
three turbines with the same type shows that the noise requirements can be diﬃcult to
be met and it restricts the wind farm layout to place the turbines in a geometric pattern.
However, if the turbine type is changed to a turbine with a smaller rotor diameter the
emission would have lower noise emission for each turbine thus making it easy to get the
desired layout.
(a) Noise results at 6 m/s
(b) Noise results for 8m/s
Figure 4.12: Noise emission from the 2 wind turbines located in Fårevejle
4.5
Hagesholm
The area in Hagesholm, shown in ﬁgure 4.13, is appointed to be a possible location for
a new wind energy project[33]. In order to secure the appointed area had passed the
requirements for a wind energy project, the same approach used in 4.3.1 and 4.4 was
carried out.
In addition, the appointed area can also be found in the map made by
plandata[17].
37
==PAGE:52==
Figure 4.13: Available area to site in Hagesholm
Like Fårevejle, this area was also a part of Lammefjorden. A very ﬂat and clear landscape
is observed. The area is close to the town of Grevinge and is surrounded by two cottage
areas [37]. The area borders to the municipality of Holbæk and has to be in contact
during the planning since it can aﬀect the surroundings of their grounds.
4.5.1
Vattenfall Windfarm
Even though the area is reserved for a wind energy project, Hagesholm has a very con-
troversial reputation. Just across the border to Holbæk municipality, Zealands biggest
wind park is located with its 10 Siemens 2.3 MW turbines. Both under the planning
and operation of the wind park a neighbourhood close to the turbines location, were very
unsatisﬁed of their appearance. Two of the complainers were Boye Jensen and his wife
whom represented the furious neighborhood [26]. The worried neighborhood had com-
plaints about the noise level emitted from the turbines. Mr. Jensen could not drive his
company properly since his employees had diﬃculty in concentrating due to the turbines.
Furthermore, he complained over not being a higher limit than 400 m from residential
to wind turbines.
However, the noise report acquired from Vattenfall indicated that
the turbines passed the noise requirements and accepted from the Holbæk municipality.
Unfortunately, according Mr. Jensen and Mrs. Jensen, they were forced to close their
business [26].
38
==PAGE:53==
Both the municipality and Vattenfall faced huge challenges during the project and plan-
ning a new wind project at the other side of the border might resurrect the old troubles
experienced in Holbæk to Odsherred.
4.5.2
Visual Impact and Landscape
The locations of the wind turbines in Hagesholm can be seen in ﬁgure 4.13. The wind
turbines are pretty close to the appointed area in Odsherred. Beside the Vattenfall wind
park another 19 year old wind park is located in between. The only advantage of this
area is its acquaintance with wind turbines. The landscape and visibility from residential
is already disturbed from the existent wind turbines in Holbæk. On the other hand, in
the Odsherred part of Hagesholm an cottage area is seen approximately 1 km away from
each corner of the appointed area and can unfortunately present big challenges regarding
the acceptance of the project.
Mitigation
A way to avoid harming the landscape seen from the two cottage areas, is to place the
wind turbines to the West in the given area shown in ﬁgure 4.13. It is important to have
the stakeholders informed during the planning process in order to avoid the issues faced
during the Vattenfall project. Oﬀering shares will ease hostile landowners.
4.5.3
Shadow Flicker
The shadow ﬂicker is studied for two wind turbines along with 24 existing turbines in
the nearby area. The results show that all guidelines are kept, though just barely as
the resident denoted BE receives a ﬂicker of 9.54 hours/year being only 6 minutes from
exceeding the guidelines. The results are illustrated graphically in ﬁgure 4.14. During all
shadow ﬂicker calculations it is observed that although the shadow ﬂicker is an important
matter to consider, it does not exceed the guidelines for the potential sites and it is
assumed that excess of the guidelines is more likely to occur at sites with a big amount
of turbines and with turbines of a larger rotor diameter. The distance from the residents
to the turbines also plays a role as the detailed results illustrates the two Siemens SWT
4.0-120 turbines denoted 1 and 2 in ﬁgure 4.14 contributes the most to the ﬂicker in total
with 11.44 hours/year and 17.30 hours/year respectively. The positioning of the turbines
according to the sun is also a key factor in contributing to the ﬂicker.
39
==PAGE:54==
Figure 4.14: Shadow ﬂicker for 2 WT in Hagesholm
4.5.4
Noise
This section deals with the noise calculations of two wind turbines in Hagesholm. There
have been a number of challenges to comply with the noise requirements as a big resi-
dential area is located nearby and the turbine has a rotor diameter of 120 m. Since the
noise emissions exceed the requirements when the turbines are placed in a close distance
to each other, the only opportunity was to place the turbines in a distance of 1.8 km
between them when using the turbine type of Siemens SWT-4.0-120. The First analy-
sis showed that when only the two turbines were considered the requirements were met.
However it is necessary to account for the nearby existing turbines when computing the
noise emissions. The noise calculation is done for two Siemens SWT-4.0-120 turbines
40
==PAGE:55==
along with 10 existing Siemens SWT-2.3-93 turbines. It should be mentioned that more
existing turbines were present close to the site which can be seen in ﬁgure 4.13 but due
to restrictions using WindPRO noise data were only accessible for the Siemens turbines.
Figure 4.15 illustrates the results for the 6 m/s and 8 m/s where the noise emissions ex-
ceed the noise requirements, thus making sure that the project would be denied. It is seen
how the noise is distributed in four areas due to the turbines location covering a larger
area where the noise is emitted, thus making it diﬃcult to comply with the requirements.
It should also be mentioned that the ﬁrst analysis on noise where only the two turbines
were considered showed that the turbines could only be placed in the locations seen in
ﬁgure 4.15 in order to comply with the requirements.
(a) Noise results at 6 m/s
(b) Noise results for 8m/s
Figure 4.15: Noise emission from the 2 wind turbines located in Hagesholm
4.6
Flora and Fauna
As mentioned in [7] the ﬂora and fauna play an important role in the EIA in order to
prevent the loss of habitat. To mitigate an environmental survey it is required to make
an thorough and more accurate investigation of the ﬂora and fauna. This consists of a
site visit and an insight of the present condition of the area. Before making a site visit to
collect data the software of plandata[17] along with Google Earth is used in order to gain
knowledge of the existing situation in the area. Figure 4.16 illustrates the area of the
41
==PAGE:56==
potential wind turbine locations. It can be seen how the protection lines for stream of
waters, forests and ancient monuments is present in the nearby areas as well as a nature
protection area. However these areas lies outside the potential area for wind turbines.
Inspecting ﬁgure 4.2 it is observed that four small areas of trees are present within the
potential site. The ﬂora and fauna might be aﬀected due to these trees in the area.
Beside the mentioned ﬂora and fauna the area have great potential for the planning of
wind turbines.
Figure 4.16: Presence of ﬂora and fauna in Siddinge fjord. Source: Plandata
4.6.1
Mitigation
In order to mitigate for the surrounding ﬂora and fauna in the nearby areas the transporta-
tion of the blades and the tower during the construction phase and the decommissioning
phase need to be planned thus the protected areas are not damaged. During these phases
cranes used for mounting the wind turbine should be considered with the focus of not
damaging the surrounding ﬂora and fauna. For the mitigation of the trees within the
potential site the location of the turbines in the area should be considered, placing the
turbines in a fair distance from the present trees and prohibiting the cranes to operate
close to the trees will reduce the impact of sustaining damage to the ﬂora and fauna.
42
==PAGE:57==
4.7
Summary
From the results obtained in the EIA study, it can be concluded that the most suitable
areas to site the upcoming turbines are located in Sidinge Fjord and Fårevejle after
considering the results achieved in noise, shadow ﬂicker and visual impact and landscape
sections. Both areas have ﬂat terrain and are used for agriculture. In addition no crowded
cottage areas are observed compared with to the other available areas. Lastly, it can be
mentioned that, the turbine sites have to be located in the same coordinates used under
the noise calculations, in order to comply with the noise requirements. The explanation
of the site location, site lay-out and turbine selection will be further elaborated in the
upcoming chapters.
43
==PAGE:58==
5. Wind Resource Assessment and Model
Calibration
To have an accurate AEP calculation, a precise WAsP model has to be set up. Diﬀerent
tools and approaches have been used to achieve the most realistic WAsP model. Six
models were built based on the existing Vestas V47-660 turbines located in Sidinge, see
ﬁgure 4.2 for locations. This chapter explains how the models were build in WAsP and
which of the models had the best AEP estimation of the existing turbines. The AEP
output of the models will be compared to the actual AEP output of the existent turbines
which is provided by the central data register[11]. Lastly a long-term energy correction
study is conducted. A detailed description of the WAsP, WAT results and the work space
hierarchies can be found in appendix A.
5.1
Vector Maps
The models are built with two diﬀerent vector maps. Each vector map consists of an
elevation and a roughness map and are created by WAsP Map Editor. How the maps
were created can be seen in appendix F.2.
5.1.1
DK99 Map
The DK99 map is a combination of scanned and hand digitized elevation and roughness
map made by Danish Geodata Agency and improved by researchers of DTU Wind Energy
department in Risø. The DK99 map of Odsherred was created by combining the provided
elevation and roughness map in WAsP Map Editor.
44
==PAGE:59==
Figure 5.1: Scanned and hand digitized vector map of Odsherred(1999)
The blue contour lines seen in ﬁgure 5.1 are the 5 m height contours from the elevation
map. The roughness map is added to the elevation map in Map Editor which can be seen
by the roughness change lines. When the map is imported to WAsP project a resource
grid can be added to a speciﬁc location, in this case Sidinge, in order to visualize the
topographical relief of the turbine site 5.2.
45
==PAGE:60==
Figure 5.2: Vector Map imported to WAsP with ressource grid showing elevation.
5.1.2
SRTM-Map
The Shuttle Radar Topography Mission data set has been implemented to the newest
version of the WAsP software. It allows the user to generate a more precise vector map
than ever. A SRTM-based Vector Map can be generated with the WAsP Map Editor
tool. The elevation and roughness maps were created individually then combined into
one map like explained in 5.1.1. The elevation map with the 5 m contours is using SRTM
version 2.1, while the roughness map uses GlobCover 2009 land cover classiﬁcation, with
a land cover to roughness translation table made for GWA by DTU Wind Energy [21].
46
==PAGE:61==
Figure 5.3: A SRTM map made with the WAsP map editor, imported to a WAsP-project.
5.2
Wind Data Analysis
The WAsP models are built with three diﬀerent wind data sets. This approach has been
used to investigate which data give the most realistic AEP estimation of the existing
turbines.
5.2.1
The Risø Mast
The Risø mast is located in DTU Risø Campus and is 125 m high. Some challenges have
been faced during the decision of which wind mast to use. There were three to choose
from; the DMI mast located in Gniben, the wind data from Hagesholm wind park and
from the Risø mast. The best solution would be to collect data from the wind park since
they are geographically close to Sidinge and have new data. Unfortunately, it was not
possible to obtain the data from Vattenfall. The mast located in the military facility of
Gniben is only 10 m in height and located 30 km away from Sidinge. Since the Risø mast
is higher and presents the same distance, it was chosen for the analysis. The data of the
Risø mast was provided for a time period of 1996-2019. However, as seen in appendix,
ﬁgure G.1 the period from 2002 to 2019 of the wind measurements was lost due to the
missing wind directions in the imported data ﬁles. The best measurements are found in
the data ﬁles ranging from 1996 to 1999 and 2001. The data can be seen in ﬁgure 5.4. In
47
==PAGE:62==
addition, the data measurements of the year 2000 was also lost due to the missing wind
directions.
(a) Wind data from 1996 to 1999
(b) Wind data, 2001
Figure 5.4: Wind data ranging 1996-1999 and 2001 from the Risø Mast, used to build WAsP model
Measurements from 1996-1999 and 2001 were used to build the model. The wind data
report of the observed wind climate (OWC) can be found in appendix G.
5.2.2
The Global Wind Atlas and Danish Wind Atlas
Both the Global Wind Atlas (GWA) and Danish Wind Atlas (DWA) use a downscaling
procedure. It begins with large scale reanalysis wind climate data and ends with micro
scale wind climate data. The large scale is located on a grid with a spacing of about 70
km. For the GWA the mesoscale model uses a grid spacing of 9 km and 5 km for the
DWA. Theoretically the mesoscale model for Denmark should be more accurate. Both
of the mesoscale models will be used to build WAsP models with both SRTM-map and
DK99 map.
5.3
Uncertainty Factors and Technical Losses
The AEP estimation of the WAsP models are without technical losses and uncertainties.
The Wind Assesment Tool (WAT) can be used to apply uncertainty factors on the WAsP
models. Table 5.1 gives an overview over the used uncertainties and technical loss for the
AEP calculations in WAT. The chosen values for uncertainty are selected on the basis of
commonly used uncertainties described in the WAsP notes[31]. Furthermore the WAsP
notes state that the aggregate uncertainty for onshore farms in Europe should be between
10 and 15%[31]. It can be determined from table 5.1 that the aggregate uncertainty for
48
==PAGE:63==
the AEP calculation lies between this interval with a uncertainty of 13.8%, therefore, it
is considered that the assumed uncertainty values are reasonable.
Uncertainty factors
Uncertainty type

Technical Losses
Value [%]
Turbine availability
3
Internal transmission loss

1

5
Table 5.2: Overview of the technical loss values used in WAT[31].
The uncertainty factors can vary from project to project. Contact to Vestas and Siemens
regarding more realistic values have been made. Due to non-disclosure agreements, the
companies were not interested to share their typically used values. However, some of these
values could be adjusted more correctly by basing them on a more site speciﬁc location
and circumstances and were estimated on the basis of the WAsP course notes [31] shown
in table 5.1. The technical losses have been adjusted more speciﬁcly to the turbine site
and can be seen in table 5.2. The turbines can be serviced during their lifetime and not
be available during this period. Based on this, the turbine availability is set to 3%. The
internal transmission loss is estimated to be 1%, since the cabling to the grid being fairly
49
==PAGE:64==
close to the site. The blade degradation is set to 1% as well since turbine blades can
become dirty over time. Hence, the total technical loss on AEP is 5% and is deemed as
reasonable, as these losses often lay in the range 5 −10% according to [31].

results are from wind assessment tool (WAT) which applies all the described uncertainty factors and technical losses
on the net AEP estimated in WAsP. The calculated WAT P50 AEP represents the most likely result, considering the
uncertainties involved at each step and assuming that they follow a normal distribution. This value is known as “P50”,
because the probability of exceeding is 50%.

to the map from 1999. The SRTM-map yields the best AEP estimation of the existing
turbines with the combination of the generalized wind climate (GWC) imported from the
Global Wind Atlas (GWA). It was assumed that the GWC imported from the Danish
Wind Atlas (DWA) would be more precise due to the grid spacing mentioned in section
5.2.2. However, the worst estimation with SRTM based model is seen with the Risø-mast.
This can be due to the mast being located 30 km away from the turbine site. The wind
faces many obstacles until it reaches the turbines which WAsP takes into account. Even
50
==PAGE:65==
though the combination of GWA and SRTM map appeared to have the best estimate,
it was preferred to have a model based on real measured data. Therefore, a long-term
energy correction will be applied for further analysis in the upcoming section.
5.5
Long-term energy correction
In this section the long-term energy correction will be studied where a deeper investigation
will be carried out to understand the deviations of the calculations made in section 5.4.
This is done by using Vindstat[23] which is method based on data gathered from the
energy production and wind measurements from the test sites located in Risø, Kegnæs
and Høvsøre. This is done in order to compute the energy content of the wind and the
wind index. The data available on vindstat is from 1983-2018 where the wind data from
DWA is from 2002-2013 and the GWA data is from 2001-2010. Lastly there is the data
from the Risø mast, ranging from 1996-1999 and a separate year in 2001. Since the DWA,
GWA and Risø data range in a diﬀerent time period, the energy correction is carried out
based on their time periods by averaging the index for that period. The equation used
to calculate the index by vindstat[23] is as follows:
Index = Actual monthly production
long term average AEP

In table 5.4 the results from the energy correction are shown. For detailed calculations

the negative deviations have reached a smaller value. The results with DK99 map still
yield the largest deviations and can therefore be excluded as an input data to WAsP.

led to the smallest deviation, however when taking the energy correction into account it
is stated that the Risø, SRTM yields the smallest deviation of −5.4%. Therefore it is
decided to use the Risø data with the SRTM map for all of the subsequent calculations
in WAsP.

6. Wind Farm Calculations
In earlier chapters, the turbine sites were located. EIA was carried out for each site
locations. With the existing turbines in the municipality, the accuracy of the diﬀerent
WAsP models was estimated. The model which gave the most realistic estimation of AEP
was selected to work with further on. In this chapter the WAsP model will be used to
make AEP calculations for diﬀerent wind turbine models. In addition, the IEC-standard
for the selected turbine will be found with WindPRO site compliance. Detailed results
of the WAsP and WAT calculations can be found in appendix A.

the fusion of Siemens and Gamesa, both WAsP and WindPRO lacked information about
Siemens Gamesa (SG) turbines. Therefore, the older Siemens turbine have been chosen
since all the new models of SG turbines were not accessible in WAsP and WindPRO
wind turbine catalog. Most of the Vestas turbines were found in the catalog but lacked

a higher energy output which could be due to the larger rotor diameter. Furthermore
it is expected that the Vestas turbine would also comply with the noise and shadow
ﬂicker requirements.
This is mainly due to the fact that new, modern turbines have
been improved. These are equipped with technology aiding in the decrease of the noise
emission as well as a smaller rotor diameter compared to the older Siemens turbines.

6.3
IEC standard
In order to ensure that the wind turbines can handle the wind conditions, a calculation
must be carried out which complies with the IEC standard to get an approval when
selecting a speciﬁc turbine. This is an important part of the planning process as the
installed turbines must withstand even extreme wind speeds and turbulence that cause
great load on the turbines. The IEC standard that is investigated is the IEC 61400-1
concerning the design requirements. Table 6.2 illustrates the parameters for the wind
55

The calculations are carried out for three Siemens SWT-4.0-120 turbines and placed in
Sidinge Fjord using the WindPRO software to perform several IEC checks. The IEC
checks that have been performed are for the terrain complexity, extreme wind, eﬀective
turbulence wind distribution, ﬂow inclination and wind shear. It should be mentioned
that some challenges have been met during the calculations as no nearby mast has been
available. Online data has been gained both for the roughness and elevation along with
meso data from EMD to generate the wind statistic using the module STATGEN in
WindPRO and afterwards used as input to the site compliance module in WindPRO.
Furthermore, the initial approach was to perform the IEC checks in WAsP engineering but
due to restrictions of the tool yielding a very high background turbulence (see appendix
A.10), this has not been possible. This could also explain some of the results in WindPRO
since the software interacts with WAsP and WENG to perform the calculations. Since
the selected turbine has a IEC class IA the calculations are performed on that basis.
Figure 6.4: Main results for site compliance

==PAGE:71==
In ﬁgure 6.4 the main results is shown. It is noticed how the extreme wind lies way
below the requirements indicating that a class III turbine could also be sited at this
location. However some concerning results are observed at the eﬀective turbulence, which
is the same warning the WAT tool showed. The results indicate that the selected IEC
standard could possibly give problems during operation. However, the indication from
WindPRO is not considered to be critical, hence, the selected IEC standard is acceptable
for these turbine sites.
In appendix D.4 the graph illustrates that the eﬀective and
background turbulence lie about 0.5 above the standard. A reason for this could be that
the lengths gained from the online data are too high. For a complete overview of the
results see appendix D.4. In order to get more precise results of the turbulence a local
measurement of the turbulence is usually performed. The approval could also be granted
by the manufacturer for a speciﬁc site in most cases. Furthermore, it is noticed from
Hagesholm that the existing 2.3 MW Siemens turbines have a IEC class IIA which can
be seen in appendix D.4 ﬁgure D.1 and therefore it is assumed that the selected turbine
will ﬁt the purpose.
